The twenty-ninth chapter.
In treating the dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow of the hand is often dislocated and when it is reset, it is easier compared to other joints. However, it should be quickened when it needs to be set back immediately before the site gets cold or when there is a hot lump present.
When resetting the dislocation, you place the forearm on a board and stretch the patient's arm with one person supporting it. The doctor then places his hand on the joint and pushes until it returns to its position. However, it is necessary to check if the dislocation has moved inside the wrist.
Let the patient place his palm on the board during stretching and contracting. If the dislocation is pronounced towards the front of the hand, let him place his backhand on the board so that the doctor's hand can rest at the same joint point. If it returns immediately, otherwise apply a compress with an analgesic for any lump.
Leave him without making him resume the position, for he cannot bear it nor can he be reset after days have passed. The joint remains in its unnatural position and this does not harm the patient except if his hand was relaxed and unable to hold anything.
Then, you will realize that the nerve has been severed or tolerate it; there is no remedy for it except to apply pressure with your hand. It may help or not at all. After successfully resetting the elbow, apply the mentioned compress then have him hold it every five days.
Then the hand is treated and practiced, if its movement fails and there appears something of the body on it, it should be moistened with heated water and vinegar until it softens.
The twenty-ninth chapter.
In treating the dislocation of fingers.
The fingers can dislocate in all directions. If one of the fingers has dislocated to the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns. Then tie the end of the finger and hang it towards the direction where it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release and stretch it until it stands firmly on the first day. If this is done at night, tie it in the same manner; she will continue to treat her by movement during the day and tie it at night, repeating this process for several days until it strengthens.
And similarly, do the same with it if it dislocates towards the palm of the hand. Tie her in the direction of that side and do what you did for your first treatment until she recovers. And do this with her whenever it dislocates to any other directions, respectively.
The thirty-first chapter.
In treating the dislocation of the shoulder blade.
If a vertebra in the shoulder blade or neck, or a complete dislocation of the vertebra occurs or if many vertebrae have been displaced from their places, there is no cure for it as death hastens towards the invalid.
That is indicated by the excrement of the sick person being uncontrollably released, unable to retain it. It often relaxes and loses some of its parts either in his legs or arm or both. If one vertebra has been displaced from its place, it tends to keep displacing.
Their diminishment is towards four directions. One in which it goes backward, it is called an ulcer. As for its treatment, if the ulcer occurred during childhood there is no cure nor exemption from it. If it occurred after falling or being hit and so on, then there is a cure.
They initially mentioned various types of treatment with extensive language, much of which was not beneficial. I say that if an ulcer occurs in front of the chest, there is no remedy or exemption from it. The same applies for ulcers on both sides as well. However, ulcers that develop on the back, especially those I am describing, can be treated. This involves covering them with a dressing.

The thirty-first chapter.
In treating the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the knee joint are only subjected to dislocation, not the slight wear or indentations that other joints may experience. The hip joint dislocates on four sides.
And that is because it can dislocate inwards and outwards, as well as forwards and backwards. However, it more often dislocates inwards than outwards or backwards. A sign of an internal dislocation is if when you compare the healthy leg to the diseased one, the diseased leg appears longer.
The knee is usually beyond the normal position. The patient cannot bend his leg at the point where it should be bent, and this point behind the bend becomes swollen and clear before the thigh bone reaches there.
The sign of the one whose jaw is exposed outward is symptoms contrary to these symptoms, and the sign of the one whose jaw is exposed forward is that he extends his leg fully until there's no bend in it unless there is pain in the knee, and if he attempts to walk, he cannot do so up ahead.
His urine is retained, and he experiences pain in his anus. When walking, he has difficulty due to the heel of his foot touching first. The sign of the one whose jaw is exposed backward is that he cannot fully extend his knee before bending his ribcage. Also, his leg length is shorter compared to the other side.
The ribcage is relaxed, and the thigh bone can be clearly seen at the place of the spine. As for the opposite cases of this jaw position, if you look closely, when the old jaw has become accustomed to its owner and hasn't tried to return to its original state and has remained in that condition, there is no definite cure.
It should not be allowed to return to its original state. As for the one who has undergone a recent jaw surgery, regardless of what type it was from the four types of jaw surgeries, prompt action should be taken to rotate and extend the joint, moving it inwards and outwards, moving it left and right; this might help.
He returned without needing any other treatment. If he doesn't return like this, it's advisable to have a strong attendant who can extend his leg from below either with their hands or with a strap tied around his thigh above the knee for another attendant to support him from above.
He should insert his hands under his armpits, then pull with a soft strap at the base of his thigh, holding onto one end of the strap. Another attendant can support him from below. The attendant can either be positioned in front, moving from the position of the ribcage to the position behind the knee, or from behind towards the back area.

The Second and Third Chapters.
In treating dislocation of the knee.
The knee dislocates on three sides: to the inside, to the outside, and downwards towards behind. It does not dislocate in front of the patella. The sign of its dislocation is that the patient instructs to bring their leg up to their groin.
If it does not stick to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. All means of putting it back should be tried until the patient sits down with their legs extended if they can do so. Sit behind them to hold him in place and gently guide him backwards for a little while, then sit on his thighs.
You should press your back against their head and place their leg between your legs. Then, hold onto their knee with your hands and interlock them on top of the knee while touching each side of it firmly. Another assistant should extend their leg until you can return the knee to its place.
The sign that it has returned is when the leg sticks to the thigh smoothly and without discomfort, then secure it and have the leg stick to the thigh. Then bind them all with a bandage for three or four days, and then release them. Only slight walking should be done until healing occurs.
If returning it as described to you is not possible, then resort to the powerful ligatures which I have provided instructions for in treating the hip until it returns.
The thirty-fourth chapter.
In the treatment of freeing the heel.
The heel may detach slightly or completely, and its dislocation can be either inward or outward. The sign of a heel dislocation is that you will see the heel protruding prominently in the direction to which it has detached.
As for the treatment of a slight detachment, it is easy to reposition; you simply need to gently guide and adjust until it returns. However, in treating complete dislocation, one should have the incapacitated person sit while being supported by a strong attendant from behind at the waist.
Then, with your right hand you grasp the top of his foot and with your left hand from below at the bottom of the foot, in the position of the pubis. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand and then your left hand twice, then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot towards the leg without force.
You perform this twice as described, then in the third time push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling on the pubis. If it returns after one or two repetitions with this technique and you see the foot level, otherwise redo the operation upon it because it will return.
If returning it as described to you is not possible, then I will have the incapacitated person lie on their back on the ground and dig down deeply into the earth with a pointed tool so that it comes between his legs. Then he wraps him tightly but not to harm the incapacitated person, and then adjusts his thigh strap.
Then a second attendant holds either his hands or binds them with a rope around the neck of the patient, then each attendant extends their arms against that of their companion and a knee is placed between the thighs of the sick person which they hold to prevent their body from sagging downwards during extension. Then the surgeon equalizes the jaw by hand.
And another attendant holds the healthy leg downwards, causing the jaw to quickly return. When the jaw returns and its return is evident, carry the dressing and pressure and tighten it with tight bindings, placing the ankle by the binding downwards. It's advisable to avoid the nerve located above the heel from behind.
So that the bandage isn't too tight and hurts him, leave it on for two or three days. If the bandage relaxes, tighten it again and release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should avoid walking for forty days.
If he walks before this time, there's no guarantee that the jaw won't break and cause harm or that he will accept treatment afterwards. If he experiences a hot lump, it may be necessary to use what we've described in previous sections of treatment and dressing until it goes away.
The fortieth chapter.
In treating dislocated fingers of the hand.
It is necessary to reposition what has dislocated from its place with a gentle push for only a short time. This is not difficult but rather easy, if the dislocation occurred in some parts of the foot, it may be required to place the sick person's leg on an even surface on the ground.
Or on a plank while standing like when walking, then you stand up place your foot where the joints are and then press it with your foot with force until it returns and appears to have returned properly in its position.
Then make a board under his foot, so that the whole foot has two ends. Then tighten it firmly for three days. Afterwards, let him rest and avoid walking for many days until it strengthens and he is ready to walk if God wills.

In the types of jaw dislocation which occurs with injury, or with fracture, or both.
